Inspectors' Office, Town Hall, St. Pancras, April mh, 1902. To Dr. J. F. J. Sykes, Medical Officer of Health. Dear Sir, I beg to report on the duties performed by me during the past year. After leaving the District work on August 19th I carried out the Special duties in connection with cases of Small-pox until October the 30th, when I was transferred to the North and East divisions as Inspector of Meat and other foods, and I submit observations on the inspections made from this date to the end of the year (December 28th, 1901). Sale of Food and Drugs Acts. I obtained 79 samples of different articles for analysis by the Public Analyst. Licensed Slaughter-houses. I made 44 inspections of the eight private slaughter-houses in the divisions, and saw 118 animals killed and dressed, and such organs as, on examination, were found to be diseased the owners surrendered to me. I have not seen any breach of the London County Council By-laws during these visits. Licensed Cowhouses. I visited the nine licensed cowsheds 38 times and found them fairly satisfactory. Owing to an intimation that a cow at one of these cowsheds was suffering with a diseased udder I attended and obtained a sample of milk from this cow in the shed for Bacteriological examination, the result of which the owner was cautioned by the Public Health Committee that the milk was unfit for food. Milkshops. I inspected 39 milkshops and with few exceptions found premises generally satisfactory. Marketing Places. I inspected the various market places 236 times, and on four occasions found articles of food unsound and unfit for food of man. I made application to the magistrates in each of these cases and obtained condemnation orders for destruction. On three occasions proceedings were taken under the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, and penalties inflicted. I am, &c., Harry Robt. Child. 85 VI.—LEGISLATION. During the year the following Acts of sanitary and administrative importance to the public health were passed:— The Isolation Hospitals Act, 1901 (1 Ed. VII., Ch. 8); an Act to amend the Isolation Hospitals Act, 1893. The Births and Deaths Registration Act, 1901 (1 Ed. VII., Ch. 26); an Act to amend the law (1836 to 1874) with respect to Districts for registration purposes and the appointment of Superintendent Registrars of Births and Deaths in certain Unions. The Intoxicating Liquors (Sale to Children) Act, 1901 (1 Ed. VII, Ch. 27) ; an Act to prevent the sale of intoxicating liquors to children. The Factory and Workshops Act, 1901 (1 Ed. VII., Ch. 22); an Act to consolidate with amendments the Factory and Workshop Acts. The Isolation Hospitals Act, 1901. This Act amends and supplements the Isolation Hospitals Act of 1893, and provides for the transfer by a local authority of hospitals for use as isolation hospitals, tor the contribution to hospitals provided by a local authority, for the power of a hospital committee to contract for hospital accommodation, for the rate of interest, for amendment as to appeals to the Local Government Board, for the amendment of the definition of local authority, for copies of orders of County Councils to be sent to the Local Government Board, and for representatives of the County Councils on hospital committees. The Act does not apply to the Metropolis. The Births and Deaths Registration Act, 1901. This Act amends the sections of the Births and Deaths Registration Acts 1836 to 1874, relating to the appointment of Superintendent Registrars of temporary districts. The Intoxicating Liquors (Sale to Children) Act, 1901. This Act makes the purchase and sale of liquors in uncorked or unsealed vessels to children under 14 years of age illegal, and provides penalties for the offence. The Factory and Workshop Act, 1901. This Act casts heavy additional responsibilities upon the Medical Officer of Health, and requires him to report each year specifically upon the administration of the Act, and to send a copy of his report to the Secretary of State. In dealing with employers and employed in various directions the Act will call for the display of a large measure of tactful resource for its smooth administration. 86 In the following summary of the provisions, the abbreviations "F. & W. A." means Factory and Workshops Act, 1901, and "P. H. L. A." means Public Health (London) Act, 1891. General Powers. Under F. & W. A. Section 155.—"The powers conferred by this Act on district councils shall he in addition to, and not in substitution for, any other powers which they may possess." Powers of Entry. (A) Under P. H. L. A. Section 10 (a) authorizes the council to enter from time to time any premises for the purpose of examining as to the existence thereon of any nuisance liable to be dealt with summarily under the Act, at any hour by day (i.e., from 6 a.m. to 9 p.m.), or in the case of a nuisance arising in respect of any business, then at any hour when that business is in progress or is usually carried on. Section 26 (2) specifically extends the powers under Section 10 for the inspection of bakehouses, provides a fine for refusal or obstruction, and gives a right to enter independently of any inquiry as to the existence of a nuisance. Powers of entry are given under Section 40 for the examination of sanitary fittings, water supply, pipes, course of drains, &c., and (under Section 82 of the Metropolis Management Act, 1855) of drains. Factories come under the definition of " premises" and may be so entered. (B) Under F. & W. A. Section 125.—"For the purpose of their duties with respect to workshops and workplaces under this Act, and under the law relating to publio health, the district council and their officers shall, without prejudice to their other powers, have all such powers of entry, inspection, taking legal proceedings, or otherwise, as an inspector under this Act." The powers of an "inspector under this Act," which are given to the officers of district councils, are by Section 119:— (1) (a) "To enter, inspect, and examine at all reasonable times, by day and night, a factory and a workshop, and every part thereof, when he has reasonable cause to believe that any person is employed therein, and to enter by day any place which he has reasonable cause to believe to be a factory or workshop; and (b) "To take with him in either case a constable into a factory or workshop in which he has reasonable cause to apprehend any serious obstruction in the execution of his duty ; and (c) "To require the production of the registers, certificates, notices, and documents kept in pursuance ot this Act, and to inspect, examine, and copy the same; and 87 (d) "To make such examination and inquiry as may be necessary to ascertain whether the enactments for the time being in force relating to public health and the enactments of the factory Act are complied with so far as respects the factory or workshop, and the persons employed therein ; and (g) "To exercise such other powers as may be necessary for carrying this Act into effect. (2) "The occupier of every factory and workshop, his agents and servants, shall furnish the means required by an inspector as necessary for an inspection or the exercise of his powers under this Act in relation to that factory or workshop." Where entry is refused, a metropolitan council may either proceed under Sections 115 and 116 of the P. H. L. A. or take proceedings to recover the penalties provided by Section 119 of the F. & W. A. An officer seeking to enter any factory, workshop, or workplace must be provided with a properly authenticated document, to be produced on demand, showing that he is duly authorised to make an inspection. Part I.—Health and Safety. (i) Health (Secs. 1—9.) Section 2 is, in the Metropolis, replaced by Section 2 (1) (g) of the P. H. L. A. The latter enacts that:— "Any factory, workshop, or workplace which is not a factory subject to the provisions of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, relating to cleanliness, ventilation, and overcrowding, and (i) is not kept in a cleanly state and free from effluvia arising from any drain water-closet, urinal, or other nuisance, or (ii) is not ventilated in such a manner as to render harmless as far as practicable any gases, vapours, dust, or other impurities generated in the course of the work carried on therein that are a nuisance or injurious or dangerous to health, or (iii) is so overcrowded while work is carried on as to be injurious or dangerous to the health of those employed therein, shall be nuisances liable to be dealt with summarily under this Act." P. H. L. A. Section 25 also enables a borough council to order any factory (not subject to the provisions of the Factory Act), workshop (not being a bakehouse) or workplace, or part thereof, to be limewashed, cleansed, or purified, when certified by the medical officer of health to be necessary for the health of the persons employed therein. Section 3 re-enacts the standard of 250 cubic feet of space for ordinary working hours and of 400 for overtime. The Secretary of State is empowered to make Special Orders increasing the allowance of cubic space— (a) where artificial light, other than electric, is employed during any period of the working time; 88 (b) for special processes of manufacture; and (c) in any workshop or workplace, not a domestic workshop, which is used for work during the day and for sleeping at night. [Note.—An Order has already been made under (c) substituting 400 cubic feet for 250 cubic feet for ordinary working hours.] A notice is to be put up in every factory and workshop indicating the number of workers who may be employed in each room. Section 4 gives the Secretary of State power to direct one of the factory inspectors to take action where any council makes default in enforcing the provisions of the F. & W. A. and P. H. A. with regard to workshops and workplaces. An inspector so authorised has all the powers under both Acts which are possessed by the council, and may recover expenses from the council, The London County Council under Sections 100 and 101 of the P. H. L. A. also possess the power of acting in default. Section 5 re-enacts the provisions directing factory inspectors to make complaints to the district council of offences in a factory or workshop which are remediable only under the P. H. L. A. If the council neglect to have such offences attended to within one month from the receipt of the complaint, the inspector may take steps to remedy the nuisance complained of, and to recover expenses from the council. Section 6 requires "a reasonable temperature"to be maintained in each room of a factory or workshop, "but the measures taken must not interfere with the purity of the air of any room in which any person is employed." The Secretary of State may by Special Order require a thermometer to be provided and kept in order in each room in any class of factory or workshop. This is intended to prevent the neglect to provide fires or other means of warming, and the use of gas ironing stoves for this purpose. It is not clear whether the council or the Home Office will enforce this section in workshops. From the wording of the third subsection of this section and of the next two sections it would appear that the latter will do so. Section 7 requires that adequate means of ventilation shall be provided and used in every room of a factory or workshop, the district council being specially directed to see that this is done in workshops. The Secretary of State is empowered to issue a Special Order prescribing what is "sufficient ventilation " for any class of factories, etc. Where structural alterations are required to provide the requisite ventilation, the occupier of the factory or workshop may make complaint to a court of summary jurisdiction, and the court may apportion the expenses between the owner and occupier of the pemises "as appears to the court to be just and equitable under the circumstances of the case, regard being had to the terms of any contract between the parties." This section would include "workplaces" under Section 2 (1) (g) of the P. H. L. A. 89 Section 8. —Where water habitually accumulates on the floor of a factory or workshop, drainage is to be provided to carry off such water. In a workshop the want of such drainage (or, rather, the accumulation of water) is to be a "nuisance" remediable under the P. II. L. A., and presumably a "workplace" can be similarly dealt with under the nuisance section. Section 9 deals with the provision of sufficient sanitary conveniences in factories and workshops. In the Metropolis this is provided by Section 38 of the P. H. L. A. The Secretary of State is authorized to fix the standard of closet accommodation required. "Workplaces" are not included in the F. & W. A., they are included in the P. H. L. A. [Section 157 of the F. & W. A. exempts "men's workshops" - i.e., workshops where women, young persons, and children are not employed —from the provisions relating to " temperature, thermometers, means of ventilation, drainage of floors. sanitary conveniences " but ventilation and the provision of sanitary conveniences can be dealt with under the P. H. L. A., Sections 2 and 38.] (II) Safety (Secs. 10—18). Section 18.— (1) A court of summary jurisdiction may, on complaint by a factory inspector, and on being satisfied that any place used as a factory or workshop, or as part of a factory or workshop, is in such a condition that any manufacturing process or handicraft carried on therein cannot be so carried on without danger to health, or to life or limb, by order, prohibit the use of that place for the purpose of that process or handicraft, until such works have been executed as are in the opinion of the court necessary to remove the danger. (2) Provided that proceedings shall not be taken under this section in cases where proceedings might be taken by or at the instance of any district council under the provision of the law relating to public health, unless the factory inspector is authorised, under Section 4. Part II. Employment (Secs. 23 — 67). Section 61 prohibits the occupier of a factory or workshop from "knowingly" allowing a woman to be employed in or on his premises within one month after her confinement. Section 62 prohibits the employment of children under the age of twelve years. The district council has no power, except by complaint to the factory inspector, to take any action when it becomes aware of an infringement of Sections 61 or 62. Part IV. Dangerous and Unhealthy Industries (Secs. 73—86). Section 73.—Medical practitioners attending any patients suffering from lead, phosphorus, arsenical, or mercurial poisoning, or anthrax "contracted in any factory or workshop," are required to notify such cases to the Chief Inspector of Factories. The occupier of the factory or workshop is to report every such case to the factory inspector and certifying surgeon for the district where the factory or workshop is situated. The Secretary of State may by Special Order add other diseases to the list for notification. The Chief Inspector of Factories will probably send notifications to medical officers of health. 90 Part V. (iii). Bakehouses (Secs. 97 — 102). Section 97 prohibits the occupation of any place as a bakehouse unless the following conditions are complied with : (a) There must be no water-closet in direct communication with or within the bakehouse; (b) Every cistern supplying water to the bakehouse must be separate from any cistern supplying water-closets ; and (c) There must be no drain-opening within the bakehouse. Section 98.—A court of summary jurisdiction may on complaint by a factory inspector or a district council fine the occupier of the bakehouse if it is in the opinion of the court "in such a state on sanitary grounds" as to be unfit for such use The court may in addition to or in lieu of the fine direct such works to be carried out as are, in the opinion of the court, necessary to remedy the conditions complained of. Section 99 requires the walls, ceilings, and all passages and staircases of a bakehouse to be either painted and varnished or limewhitened. If painted, they are to be washed once in every six months, and repainted once at least in every seven years. If limewhitened, the limewash must be renewed once at least in every six months. Section 100.—Any place on a level with the bakehouse may not be used for sleeping purposes unless the following conditions are complied with : (a) It must be shut off from the bakehouse by a partition extending from floor to ceiling ; (b) It must have a window communicating with the external air of at least nine square feet, of which at least four and a half square feet must be made to open. Section 101.—An underground bakehouse not in use as a bakehouse at the passing of the F. & W. A. (August 17, 1901) shall not be used again as a bakehouse, and no new bakehouse can be opened after that date. After January 1, 1901, no underground bakehouse may be used "unless certified by the district council to he suitable " for such use. "An underground bakehouse shall not be certified as suitable unless the district council is satisfied that it is suitable as regards construction, light, ventilation, and in all other respects." In the event of a council refusing to certify, the occupier may appeal " within twenty-one days from the refusal" to a court of summary jurisdiction, when "if it appears to the satisfaction of the court that the bakehouse is suitable for use," as regards construction, light, etc., the court may grant a certificate. Where structural alterations are required to obtain the certificate, the occupier may recover part of the costs from the owner in the manner set out under Section 7 of the Act. Section 102 directs the district council to enforce the provisions of Sections 97 to 101, but in London this section is replaced by Section 26 of the P. H. L. A. 91 While Section 102 limits the powers of the district council to “retail bakehouses Section 28 of P. H. L. A. read with Section 141 of the same Act, gives the metropolitan borough councils jurisdiction over all bakehouses, i.e., wholesale and retail, which are not “factories.” Part V. (iv.). Laundries (Sec. 103). Section 103.—Laundries are neither factories nor workshops, but as to sanitary requirements they are to be dealt with as if in either one or the other category, according to the presence or absence of mechanical power, except laundries where the only persons employed are— “(a) inmates of any prison, reformatory, or industrial school, or other institution for the time being subject to inspection under any Act other than this Act; or “(b) inmates of an institution conducted in good faith for religious or charitable purposes; or “(c) members of the same family dwelling there, or in which not more than two persons dwelling elsewhere are employed.” Where no mechanical power is used laundries are treated as “workshops,” and are therefore under the supervision of the local authority. In the Metropolis, having regard to Section 155 of the Factory Act, the district councils retain control over laundries under the provisions of the P. H. L. A. Part VI.—Home Work (Sec. 107-115). Section 107.—This section is to be applied to such trades as the Secretary of State may by Special Order direct. Occupiers of factories and workshops and contractors employed by such occupiers are to keep lists of the people working for them “outside the factory or workshop,” together with the addresses of the places where such people work. Copies of such lists are to be sent to the council of the district where the factory or workshop concerned is situated “on or before” the 1st days of February or August in each year. The council of each district is required to analyze the lists and to distribute the names and addresses of those persons not actually working in the district to the councils of the districts where the work is actually carried on. The council is also to keep a record of the lists and of the addresses distributed, and such records are to be open to inspection by the factory inspector. The lists kept by the occupier of the factory or workshop are to be open to inspection by any duly authorized officer of the district council. Section 103.—The application of this section is by Special Order of the Secretary of State. If the district council is of opinion that any place where work is carried on outside a factory or workshop “is injurious or dangerous to the health of the persons employed therein,” and give notice thereof to the occupier of the factory or workshop for whom such work is done, then, if such occupier continue, after the expiration of one month from the date of the 92 notice, to give out work to be done in such unhealthy place, he will be liable, on complaint to a court of summary jurisdiction, to a fine not exceeding £10. A contractor giving out work is under the same restriction as the occupier of a factory or workshop. Section 109.—An occupier of a factory or workshop, or any contractor employed by such occupier, who knowingly causes or allows wearing apparel to be made, cleaned, or repaired in any dwelling-house or building connected therewith when any inmate of such dwelling-house is suffering from small-pcx or scarlet fever, will be liable to a fine not exceeding £10, “unless he proves that he was not aware of the existence of the illness in the dwelling-house, and could not reasonably have been expected to become aware of it.” Section 110.—This section applies to “the making, cleaning, washing, altering, ornamenting, finishing, and repairing of wearing apparel, and any work incidental thereto, and such other classes of work as may be specified by Special Order of the Secretary of State.” The diseases referred to in this section are those included in the schedule for notification as from time to time varied or altered. A district council may forbid work to be given out to any person living or working in any dwelling-house where any infectious disease occurs. The order may be served on the occupier of the factory or workshop whence the work is given out, or on the contractor employed by such occupier. The order may apply to the whole of the house or to any part thereof, and may be made even if the patient has been removed from the house. The order is to either specify a definite period, or to be subject to the condition of disinfection to the satisfaction of the medical officer of health of that part of the house which is liable to be infected, or to “other reasonable precautions “being adopted. The order may “in any case of urgency” be issued on the authority of any two or more members of the council acting on the advice of the medical officer of health. Contravention of any order issued under this section entails a penalty not exceeding £10. Home Work Order. In pursuance of the powers conferred by the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, the Home Secretary has made the following Order, which may be cited as the Home Work Order, 1901, (11th December) and which came into force on January 1st, 1902. I. Section 107 of the Act (relating to lists of outworkers) and Section 108 (relating to employment in unwholesome premises) shall apply to the following classes of work: The making, cleaning, washing, altering, ornamenting, finishing, and repairing of wearing apparel and any work incidental thereto; the making, ornamenting, and finishing of lace and of lace curtains and nets; cabinet and furniture making and upholstery work; the making of electro-plate; the making of files; and fur-pulling. II. Section 110 (relating to the prohibition of home work in places where there is an infectious disease) shall apply to the following classes of work: The making, cleaning, washing altering, ornamenting, finishing, and 93 repairing of wearing apparel and any work incidental thereto (as in the said section specified); the making, ornamenting, and finishing of lace and of lace curtains and nets; upholstery work; and fur-pulling. The lists of out-workers required to be kept by Section 107 and the copies thereof are to be kept and made in the form and manner and with the particulars shown in the schedule annexed to the Order. The Order of March 23, 1898, relating to lists of out-workers is revoked. Section 111.—Domestic factories and workshops are exempt from the requirements of the F. & W. A. as to means of ventilation, drainage of floors, and thermometers. (See also next section.) Section 112 annuls the exemption granted by the preceding section in domestic factories and workshops where any manufacture or process is carried on which has been certified by the Secretary of State to be “dangerous.” Section 113 empowers the Secretary of State to give notice as to the provisions of the Act which are to be enforced in domestic factories and workshops. Section 114.—Straw-plaiting, pillow-lace-making, glove-making, and any other light handicraft specially added to the list by the Secretary of State, when carried on for profit in any private house or room, are not to constitute such house or room a workshop. Any house or room where work is carried on by the occupants of such house or room at irregular intervals, or where such work does not furnish the whole or principal means of living of the family, is to be similarly exempt. Such places will still come within the P. II. L. A. Part VIII.—Administration (Secs. 118—134). Section 127.—Notice of the opening of a new factory or workshop is to be given by the occupier thereof to the district factory inspector within one month of the day of commencing to occupy such place. The inspector is to inform the council of the district in which any new workshop is situated of such opening. Section 129 requires the occupier of every factory and workshop to keep a “general register,” in which are to be entered particulars of children and young persons employed therein; of the periodical limewashing; and of other matters. “Where any entry is required by this Act to be made in the general register, the entry made by the occupier shall, as against him, be admissible as prima facie evidence of the facts therein stated, and the failure to make any entry so required with respect to the observance of any provision of this Act shall be evidence that that provision has not been observed.” It is not clear whether the district council has the power to demand the production of the register, except possibly as regards limewashing of bakehouses under Section 26 of the Health Act. The words “or otherwise” in Section 125 of the Factory Act are, however, so comprehensive 94 that it may be well to assume such power until the contrary has been decided by the court. The provision of this section does not apply to “men's workshops.” Section 131. — “Every district council shall keep a register of all workshops situate within their district.” The Home Office will afford facilities to district councils to compile their first registers. Section 132.— “The medical officer of health of every district council shall, in his annual report to them, report specifically on the administration of this Act in workshops and workplaces, and he shall send a copy of his annual report, or so much of it as deals with this subiect, to the Secretary of State.” Section 133 requires the medical officer of health to notify the district factory inspector of the employment of women, young persons and children in those factories, workshops, etc., where the abstracts required under the F. & W. A. to be affixed are not displayed. Definitions. “Child”—Anyone under the age of fourteen years, who is thirteen years old, but has not obtained the school certificate of proficiency or attendance (F. & W. A., Sec. 156). “Young person”—One who has ceased to be a child, but is under the age of eighteen years ” (F. & W. A., Sec. 156). “Woman”—A female of the age of eighteen years and upwards (F. & W. A., Sec. 156). “District Council”—As regards the Metropolis outside the City, the council of a metropolitan borough (F. & W.A., Sec. 153). “Factory”—(a) Any place where steam, water, or other mechanical power is used in connection with any trade process (F. & W. A., Sec. 149). (b) All places where the industries mentioned in Part I. of Schedule VI. are carried on, whether mechanical power be or be not used. “ Workshop” - (a) Any place, which is not a factory, where manual labour is exercised by way of trade (F. & W. A., Sec. 149). (b) The places specified in Part II. of Schedule VI., when mechanical power is not used therein. Schedule VI, Part II., contains a list of “non-textile factories and workshops.” They include hat works, rope works (rope walks), bakehouses, lace warehouses, shipbuilding yards, quarries (not being mines), drycleaning, carpet-beating, and bottle-washing works. “Tenement workshop”—A workshop jointly occupied by independent workers (F. & W. A., Sec. 149). 95 “Domestic factory,” “Domestic workshop”—A private room or dwelling where a trade process is carried on by the members of the family residing therein (F. & "W. A., Sec. 115). “Workplace”— “A place or premises shall not be excluded from the definition of a factory or workshop by reason only that the place or premises is or are in the open air” (F. & W. A., Sec. 149). In Bennett v. Harding (1900, 2 Q.B 397) it was held that the term “workplace” was not to be limited to places where manufacturing processes were carried on, but should include any “place where work is done permanently and where people assemble together to do work permanently of some kind or other.” “Bakehouse”—Any place in which are baked bread, biscuits, or confectionery, from the baking or selling of which a profit is derived (P. H. L. A., Sec. 141). “Underground bakehouse”—A bakehouse any baking room of which is so situate that the floor is three or more feet below the surface of the footway of the adjoining street, or of the ground adjoining or nearest to the room (F. & W. A., Sec. 101). . “Baking room”—Any room used for baking or for. any process incidental thereto (F. & W. A., Sec. 101). DEFINITION OF UNDERGROUND ROOM AND UNDERGROUND BAKEHOUSE. The following is the opinion given by Messrs. Cunliffes and Davenport on the 9th November, 1901, upon a report made by me upon this subject. Underground rooms. The definition of an underground room is now entirely derived from the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, Section 96. By sub-section (9) of that Section an underground room includes any room of a house, the surface of the floor of which room is more than three feet below the surface of the footway of the adjoining street or of the ground adjoining or nearest to the room. By sub-section (C) every underground room has to be provided with an open area as therein prescribed, upwards from six inches below the level of the floor. The ground “adjoining or nearest to the room” must, we think, mean grouud other than such area, and would include back garden, or back yard, or any other neighbouring surface other than such area. The result is that in our opinion unless the floor of a back room is more than three feet below the surface of the adjoining ground as above defined, it is not an underground room. 96 Underground Bakehouses. An underground bakehouse to come within Section 101 of the factory and Workshop Act, 1901, after the 4th January, 1901, must be a room so situate that the surface of the floor is more than three feet below the surface of the footway of the adjoining street or of the ground adjoining or nearest to the room. There is no provision for an adjoining area for a bakehouse, and we can only suppose that the surface from which the three feet must be measured where the footway of the adjoining street is not applicable, must be that of the ground physically nearest to the room, on whatever side of it. There seems likely to be uncertainty as to this, but each case will, we think, have to be decided on its own merits. Weatheritt v. Cantlay. Houses let in Lodgings. This case was decided in the King's Bench Divisional Court on May 13th, 1901. The late Vestry of St. George-the-Martyr, by their Sanitary Inspector Weatheritt, summoned the Defendant Cantlay for non-compliance with an Order of the Vestry to furnish information to enable the Authority to register a block of artisan's dwellings as a lodging house, and on the hearing before Mr. Slade, the Police Magistrate, the summons was dismissed. On the application of the Vestry a case was stated for the decision of the High Court and came before two Judges, who referred it to the tribunal above-mentioned. After hearing the arguments, the Lord Chief Justice in giving Judgment said: “I think, now this case has been argued out, that there is really no difficulty in it. I do not think that any of the cases on the meaning of the word ‘house’ for the purpose of particular Statutes help us. I think we have really to look to see what the object of this legislation was, and then construe the words with reference to the object of the legislation. By Sub-section A of Section 91 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891: 'Every Sanitary Authority shall make and enforce such Bye-laws as are requisite for the following matters —that is to say: (a) For fixing the number of persons who may occupy a house or part of a house wbhch is let in lodgings.' I think the object of this legislation was to control the number of people who should occupy a tenement which may fairly be described as a separate house, and that is shown by the words ' part of a house which is let in lodgings.' Speaking for myself, I do not draw any distinction between the words 'let in lodgings' or occupied by members of more than one family,' except that one state of facts may bring it within one set of words, and another state of facts may bring it within the other, but whether the lodgings are furnished or unfurnished for the purpose of this Section seems to me to make no difference. I think I must find, in order to bring it within the Section, either a house which is itself let in lodgings or a part of a house which is let in lodgings. It is said, when you come to deal with the facts of this case that you are to look at the whole structure and regard the whole building of bricks and mortar as one house, and then, if you find it sub-divided inside, it is a house which is, or a part of which is, let in lodgings. I do not call attention to the extraordinary result of that argument except for the purpose of trying to see why the Legislature should have put in those limiting words, but one cannot help seeing that if Mr. Macmorran's view is right there might be some very large structure of absolutely separate resident al flats, and if any one of them was let in lodgings the whole of them, 97 must come in for the purpose of this. Further than that, I cannot help thinking1 that there is a good deal in the view that, although the Legislature might have imposed a limit to the value in every case, either lodgings let at a certain rent or houses occupied by members of more than one family or a house that is to say a separate tenement let at a certain rent; yet, still, when you find them applicable to a given subject matter, and not to a subject matter limited by some pecuniary standard. I think when you look at the facts of this case this building is a collection of houses for the purpose of this Section. There is no front door.* There is a common passage and common staircases. On those common passages and common staircases open a number of front doors which may be locked, and are separately occupied by the tenants of those. If any one of those is let in lodgings, or if a room is occupied by members of more than one family then the particular landlord of that particular house would come within the terms of the Section. In my opinion it is going too far to say that the whole structure of bricks and mortar is a house, or part of a house let in lodgings or occupied by members of more than one family, simply because there are separate tenements and separate occupations under the circumstances stated in this case. I think, therefore, the judgment of the Magistrate was quite right." The other Justices concurred, and the appeal of the Vestry—now merged in the Metropolitan Borough of Southwark—was dismissed with costs. Hedley v. Webb. Combined Drainage of Semi-detached Houses. The word "drain" means any drain of and used for the drainage of one building only, or premises within the same curtilage, and made merely for the purpose of communicating therefrom with a cesspool or other like receptacle for drainage, or with a sewer into which the drainage of two or more buildings or premises occupied by different persons is conveyed. "Sewer" includes sewers and drains of every description, except drains to which the word "drain" interpreted as aforesaid applies. In this case it was decided in the Chancery Division by Mr. Justice CozensHardy on 26th March, 1901, that the term "one building" was not equivalent to the term "one house," that a pair of semi-detached houses only constituted one building, and that a pipe constructed to carry away the drainage of two semi-detached houses to the main sewer is a drain used for the drainage of one building only and does not rest with the Local Authority.
